  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4851da31-8111-478b-894f-7be5868cfd87'
      });

<div id="review_334652043" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/334652043" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1433865-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1254324661p2/1433865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/334652043?book_show_action=true">May 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/1433865-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345435248" href="/book/show/76720.Darwin_s_Radio">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1433865-robert?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer334652043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15362588422271566297">So I keep on reading Bear novels, feeling disappointed, waiting a while, then rinse and repeat.<br/><br/>This time I've clarified why I am so ambivalent about this guy: he has fascinating ideas then writes dull books about them. The premise here is an extreme example. Our "junk" DNA turns out to be a collection of emergency rapid-response evolutionary accelerators - and the emergency response has just been triggered. Cue mysterious pregnancies, peculiar facial mutations and a really big scientific mystery</span>
  <span id="freeText15362588422271566297" style="display:none">So I keep on reading Bear novels, feeling disappointed, waiting a while, then rinse and repeat.<br /><br />This time I've clarified why I am so ambivalent about this guy: he has fascinating ideas then writes dull books about them. The premise here is an extreme example. Our "junk" DNA turns out to be a collection of emergency rapid-response evolutionary accelerators - and the emergency response has just been triggered. Cue mysterious pregnancies, peculiar facial mutations and a really big scientific mystery that turns very political very fast. The detail is very convincing - Bear did a heap of research.<br /><br />But here's the problem: almost every event of a dramatic nature happens off-stage and the middle part of the book, between the initial scientific drama and the political nightmare at the end bogs down severely. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Then, to add insult to injury, the book closes before the new generation of evolved humans reaches their teens, so the social consequences are not fully explored (but there is a sequel). It looks like things are heading into X-Men territory, but of course more seriously treated, or, more precisely, in the vein of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/21158.Nancy_Kress" title="Nancy Kress" rel="nofollow noopener">Nancy Kress</a>'s Sleepless books. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />There is a theme of the disaster that occurs when science gets forced into the political arena; you only have to look at the climate change debate to know how that goes. It is very realistically handled but develops too slowly. I am reminded of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1858.Kim_Stanley_Robinson" title="Kim Stanley Robinson" rel="nofollow noopener">Kim Stanley Robinson</a>. Several of his works deal with science and internal and external politics and how real science is done and I can't help thinking a more interesting novel would have resulted if he had started with the same material.<br /><br />I acquired <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64924.Darwin_s_Children" title="Darwin's Children by Greg Bear" rel="nofollow noopener">Darwin's Children</a> without realising that it was a sequel and then picked up this book subsequently. I will probably read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64924.Darwin_s_Children" title="Darwin's Children by Greg Bear" rel="nofollow noopener">Darwin's Children</a> at some point, since it is lying around and because it really ought to cut to the chase, with the background already painted in with excessive attention to detail but I shall try to resist the urge to buy any more Bear novels regardless of how interesting the premise sounds...</span>
  <a data-text-id="15362588422271566297" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_570477096" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570477096" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570477096?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=mindfuq">mindfuq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=biopunk">biopunk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=top-one-hundred">top-one-hundred</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570477096" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17999077069959491409">The first time I read this I felt horrified and dazed for weeks. I still consider this a masterpiece of horror/sci-fi. The characters are somewhat memorable, but more memorable is their pain; indeed, the pain of the whole world was felt in the back of my mouth, preparing it rise up from my stomach, up the pipe, out the maw, to hang onto my lip and smack me thrice on my face, wink, and then jump off to slither under the door-jam and horrify someone else.<br/><br/>Don't get me wrong, this is a pure sci-fi n</span>
  <span id="freeText17999077069959491409" style="display:none">The first time I read this I felt horrified and dazed for weeks. I still consider this a masterpiece of horror/sci-fi. The characters are somewhat memorable, but more memorable is their pain; indeed, the pain of the whole world was felt in the back of my mouth, preparing it rise up from my stomach, up the pipe, out the maw, to hang onto my lip and smack me thrice on my face, wink, and then jump off to slither under the door-jam and horrify someone else.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong, this is a pure sci-fi novel, but no sci-fi affects me as much as the types that are just as facile in other genres. This one does and gleefully so. I may not know that much about biology, or enough to tear Bear apart, but I followed his arguments and treatment and was amazed at the way he pulled a rabbit out of the junk DNA.<br /><br />I've been a fan of Greg Bear's work for many years, and I thought I had really loved works like Eon and Legacy, and then I was amazed by Queen of Angels and then I was jumping up and down with Moving Mars. His short story collection of Tangents still makes me sit in awe. Still, all of these books paled in comparison with Darwin's Radio.<br /><br />I have to say one thing: I cried uncontrollable tears at no less than three times during this novel. I cannot give higher praise.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="17999077069959491409" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_86234971" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/86234971" />
    <a title="kenzimone" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2247342-kenzimone"><img alt="kenzimone" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343206833p2/2247342.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/86234971?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="kenzimone" class="user" itemprop="url" name="kenzimone" href="/user/show/2247342-kenzimone">kenzimone</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345435248" href="/book/show/76720.Darwin_s_Radio">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">No one</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=law-medical">law-medical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=geek-talk">geek-talk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=misc-creatures">misc-creatures</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=investigative">investigative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=awful">awful</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=i-own-it">i-own-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=1-star">1-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2247342-kenzimone?shelf=read-2010">read-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer86234971&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86234971" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8676769134601033389"><br/><br/>I did not enjoy this book in the slightest. <br/><br/>I probably should have seen it coming, what with the very first sentence of the very first chapter likening the color of the sky backdrop of the alps to 'a dog's pale crazy eye'. Even when, on the very next page, Bear described a frozen waterfall as 'a gnome's upside-down castle' I thought <i>oh, this won't be so bad</i>.<br/><br/>I was wrong. Dead wrong.<br/><br/>First, let's talk about geek talk. I'm a big fan of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5194.Michael_Crichton' title='Michael Crichton' rel='nofollow noopener'>Michael Crichton</a>, and as such I expect a book's geek talk to be </span>
  <span id="freeText8676769134601033389" style="display:none"><br /><br />I did not enjoy this book in the slightest. <br /><br />I probably should have seen it coming, what with the very first sentence of the very first chapter likening the color of the sky backdrop of the alps to 'a dog's pale crazy eye'. Even when, on the very next page, Bear described a frozen waterfall as 'a gnome's upside-down castle' I thought <i>oh, this won't be so bad</i>.<br /><br />I was wrong. Dead wrong.<br /><br />First, let's talk about geek talk. I'm a big fan of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5194.Michael_Crichton" title="Michael Crichton" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Crichton</a>, and as such I expect a book's geek talk to be nicely interwoven into the regular dialog and not be too over the top for me, as a non-scientist, to handle. An author needs to be very talented to accomplish this, and he must also be able to understand the importance of dumbing it down a notch (but not too much!) and definitely not going on for pages upon pages.<br /><br />Which is what Bear does. He <i>just won't stop</i>. I ended up skimming the sections where the geek talk occurred because it was simply <i>that boring</i>.<br /><br />It should also be pointed out that Bear actually namedrops Michael Crichton in the middle of the book. Only, instead of handling it with class Crichton's cast as a celebrity spokesperson for the Evil Corporation who wants to kill babies. Nice.<br /><br />Then there's the offensive bits.<br /><br />First, we're informed that if you study Science you too can be rid of your silly belief in God! A few premed courses will leave you happily content and 'dubious of' your 'religious upbringing' (page 390). Lovely.<br /><br />Following that, there's a scene where someone compares being forced to sign up for a national database to the persecution of the Jews during WWII. It came off as really tasteless, and I definitely reacted to it, but I figured that maybe it was just a case of bad wording?<br /><br />Then came this: <blockquote>"They stared at me in the market," Kaye said. "I felt like a leper. Worse, like a <i>nigger</i>." (page 516)</blockquote>WHAT IN THE WORLD?! With the deliberate emphasis and everything! My face went all D: because HOLY HELL you did not just state that being black is worse than having a chronic disease which can cause permanent damage to your face and make your limbs turn numb and/or diseased!<br /><br />Then comes the blow to my taste as a reader, because the last fifty pages of the book is all about Kaye and her mutant baby pregnancy and the birth of said mutant child, who they name Stella Nova because Stella and Nova both mean ~star~.<br /><br />Oh, and yeah, the baby can speak from birth, is telepathic, is the most beautiful thing ever and has pretty spots of colors on her cheeks and in her eyes that can change color like so: <blockquote>Stella Nova sent waves of fawn and gold over her cheeks... (page 505)</blockquote>Also, she grows and develops with super human speed. I felt like I was reading <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41865.Twilight__Twilight___1_" title="Twilight (Twilight, #1) by Stephenie Meyer" rel="nofollow noopener">Twilight</a>
</i>.<br /><br />I could mention the blandness of the stereotypical main female character (who's trapped in a man's world and cries a lot) and the boring main male lead, but I figure that's a given.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8676769134601033389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText975482819550161920" style="display:none">An excellent idea sadly marred by poor writing, the impression is that Greg Bear came up with a great idea for a novel, researched it and then decided to tell everyone look at what I have learned. <br />The main problem is the there is a distinct clumpiness to the story a few pages of story followed by look at what I learned today, a rushed ending just as the book begins to take shape.<br /><br />It borderlines on being turgid. If we look at Andy Weir's The Martian, which is undeniably a well written novel, it contains a excellent balance of science and storytelling that makes the book a pleasure to read in quiet an addictive way.<br /><br />this book became so leaden in the middle that I took time out and read Weird Things Customers Say In Book Shops by Jen Campell and Fifty Sheds of Grey By C. T. Grey for some light relief and begun reading How The French Won Waterloo (Or Think They Did) by Stephen Clarke.<br /><br />NO this book does not inspire me to read the sequel, Darwin's Children or any of his other work, in fact this was purchased in 1999 and now I think perhaps it should have been left on the shelf for another seventeen years.</span>

<div id="review_433114103" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/433114103" />
    <a title="Amber" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13588004-amber"><img alt="Amber" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487392722p2/13588004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/433114103?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amber" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amber" href="/user/show/13588004-amber">Amber</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13588004-amber?shelf=started">started</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13588004-amber?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer433114103" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17626134007380549225">An interesting look at what might possibly be the next stage of evolution. Greg Bear's Hugo nominee is a wonderful mix of scientific and political thriller as well as a study of human reactions and relationships. Beautifully laid out and written in an interesting manner.<br/><br/>After I finished this book I sat back and thought, my god, I know all about viruses and diseases and retroviruses now. Greg Bear does not dumb down the science to make sure his audience gets it, instead he explains everything sev</span>
  <span id="freeText17626134007380549225" style="display:none">An interesting look at what might possibly be the next stage of evolution. Greg Bear's Hugo nominee is a wonderful mix of scientific and political thriller as well as a study of human reactions and relationships. Beautifully laid out and written in an interesting manner.<br /><br />After I finished this book I sat back and thought, my god, I know all about viruses and diseases and retroviruses now. Greg Bear does not dumb down the science to make sure his audience gets it, instead he explains everything several times in innovative ways to make sure the reader comprehends the importance of his storyline. The science in this book is complex and believable, compelling and worthy. While I am generally a physics and chemistry lover, the biology and molecular sciences portrayed in Darwin's Radio excited me. These aren't the same biology principles I was bored with in high school, these are full out edges of possibility, dangerous and life-changing sciences.<br /><br />The principle behind this book is that subspeciation and thus, evolution, is actually a function of biologically engineered retroviruses- retroviruses with networks to tell when a mutation is working and when one is failing. While it is generally speculative science, it is very grounded in modern principles which are explained throughout the novel as well as in a primer at the end.<br /><br />Well worth a read! This scifi book breaks the boundaries of simple outbreak thriller into the bounds of political intrigue and romance.<br /><br />(I started rereading this, but couldn't bring myself to finish it for some reason. There is an obvious change in narrative about 4/5th of the way through the book that really jarred me, where they start a road trip and it switches from science-driven narrative to meandering emotion-based drama. I just wasn't in the mood for that, but I still think overall this book is a winner.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17626134007380549225" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100858305-posthuman?shelf=suspense">suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100858305-posthuman?shelf=hard-sf">hard-sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100858305-posthuman?shelf=prehistory">prehistory</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100858305-posthuman?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/100858305-posthuman?shelf=distant-past">distant-past</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2927887137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15319598994006633333">Let's hark back to the halcyon early 2000s, when Bill Cosby might have starred in a PSA about a disease outbreak intended to calm the frightened public, when the scientific community might have accepted that an ancient virus was the evolutionary catalyst for <i>Homo sapiens</i> to subspeciate from <i>Homo neanderthalensis</i> in one generation, before we got a better handle on how screwed up the hominid family tree really is.<br/><br/>I read the sequel <i>Darwin's Children</i> about a decade ago and only recently realized I c</span>
  <span id="freeText15319598994006633333" style="display:none">Let's hark back to the halcyon early 2000s, when Bill Cosby might have starred in a PSA about a disease outbreak intended to calm the frightened public, when the scientific community might have accepted that an ancient virus was the evolutionary catalyst for <i>Homo sapiens</i> to subspeciate from <i>Homo neanderthalensis</i> in one generation, before we got a better handle on how screwed up the hominid family tree really is.<br /><br />I read the sequel <i>Darwin's Children</i> about a decade ago and only recently realized I completely missed out on the first book in the series. In my admittedly foggy recollection, the sequel was a more enjoyable read which I zipped through within a couple days. As for <i>Darwin's Radio</i>, this is an imaginative, sweeping technothriller epic with a cast of fascinating characters and exhaustively researched, brilliant (if outdated) plot. So why was it such a slow, plodding experience compared to the sequel? <br /><br />I'd say this one is simply a bit too long. There are a few chapters that get bogged down in trivial details or irrelevant background scenes that felt like the perfect spot to put the book down for a while. Barring these slow sections, the narrative flows like more of a page-turner, though there is also a disconnect with some unexplained fantasy mysticism grafted on top of meticulously researched hard science.<br /><br />As one tends to find in thrillers featuring global disasters, the action cuts between the POV scenes of various scientists and government officials in order to maintain suspenseful pacing. Kaye Lang is a brilliant molecular biologist caring for a mentally ill husband while researching endogenous retroviruses (ancient diseases encoded in our own DNA). Mitch Rafelson is the maverick pariah of anthropologists, discredited for stealing prehistoric remains from a Native American tribe. And Christopher Dicken is a lonely, driven "virus hunter" at the CDC, investigating the pathogen responsible for "Herod's Flu," a mysterious epidemic causing miscarriages in pregnant women around the world.<br /><br />Kaye's paper predicting the very sort of dormant retrovirus linked to the epidemic puts her in the spotlight. Public hysteria reaches a fever pitch as an entire generation of babies is in peril. (This is where Bill Cosby enters the picture.)<br /><br />Mitch discovers the mummified remains of a Neanderthal couple with symptoms of Herod's Flu who appear to be the parents of a modern human fetus. He is convinced these mummies can prove this is not a disease, but the next step in human evolution. Unfortunately, he gets locked out of his own discovery and shunned by the scientific community. <br /><br />One section of the book that needed trimming is a protracted climb in the Alps where Mitch searches for the Neanderthal cave while navigating a love triangle with mountaineers Tilde and Franco. This was an interminably tedious account of every single step and foothold while climbing a mountain, peppered with lots of mountaineer jargon for every imaginable type of geological formation and equipment. Either Greg Bear is an expert mountaineer, or he researches like a demon. Maybe this part was like porn for readers who are pro mountain climbers, but as a layperson I was bored to tears and ready to gouge my eyes out.<br /><br />As you might expect, the storylines of Kaye, Mitch and Christopher collide in a huge revelation and it appears the fate of humanity lies in the hands of our protagonists. Overall it's an enjoyable read, but in hindsight I wish I had skimmed some of the sluggish sections.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15319598994006633333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1085207516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer609124556513085997">As warned by a friend, the ideas here are pretty fascinating -- the book might be fifteen years behind in terms of science, but there's nothing inherently ridiculous about the idea based on the scientific knowledge of the time -- but the actual narrative is pretty deadly boring. Some of the writing is just... why would you let that slip past, editor? Hard SF isn't just about the cool ideas: there has to be some element of execution there as well, or there's no point in writing it as a novel -- t</span>
  <span id="freeText609124556513085997" style="display:none">As warned by a friend, the ideas here are pretty fascinating -- the book might be fifteen years behind in terms of science, but there's nothing inherently ridiculous about the idea based on the scientific knowledge of the time -- but the actual narrative is pretty deadly boring. Some of the writing is just... why would you let that slip past, editor? Hard SF isn't just about the cool ideas: there has to be some element of execution there as well, or there's no point in writing it as a novel -- there'd be a non-fiction audience for speculation about the future too, undoubtedly.<br /><br />It's pretty unfortunate, since Bear did the work here in setting up the world, figuring out the details, making A lead to B without a gap in logic. Unfortunately, the prose is flat, most of the characters likewise, and isn't there a song with lyrics that go <i>I don't care</i> a lot? Because it's in my head right now.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10828555558415254695">The first 200 pages or so of this book are incredibly engaging and interesting. I wasn't put off by the science talk, though there was too much of it -someone who truly understood it would probably find a lot of holes in it, and someone who didn't get it beyond the basics didn't really need to read so extensively about it- but after the first half, the book starts taking a plunge south.   <span id="freeText10828555558415254695" style="display:none">The first 200 pages or so of this book are incredibly engaging and interesting. I wasn't put off by the science talk, though there was too much of it -someone who truly understood it would probably find a lot of holes in it, and someone who didn't get it beyond the basics didn't really need to read so extensively about it- but after the first half, the book starts taking a plunge south. I stopped caring about the characters at some point in the middle, the female lead turning into quite a trope by the the end, the ending was completely unsatisfying and left a bunch of story lines hanging, plot holes and rambling side stories that never saw any closure. It was like Bear got tired of writing the story and just wanted to finish it, so he glossed over a lot of the things he had so carefully set. <br /><br />The sad part is that the concept is great and the human side of the thriller is very compelling, but once you start noticing the vices in the story, it kind of goes downhill from there. <br /><br />Total clothes fetish, by the way. What it is with describing every piece of garment?? </span>

  <span id="freeText18222492081682938034" style="display:none">This book was quite a mixed bag. It’s very hard science. There’s also a lot of politics.     <a title="Edmund Bloxam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51960700-edmund-bloxam"><img alt="Edmund Bloxam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476679086p2/51960700.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10298997890321078881">Frighteningly dull. Perhaps the most pointless succession of words ever committed to the page.<br/><br/>The blurb at the back of the book, the concept is quite interesting. Unfortunately, this is it in terms of plot. All of this detail is established around page 50 or so.<br/><br/>What happens in the monstrous, monstrous morass that follows?   <span id="freeText10298997890321078881" style="display:none">Frighteningly dull. Perhaps the most pointless succession of words ever committed to the page.<br /><br />The blurb at the back of the book, the concept is quite interesting. Unfortunately, this is it in terms of plot. All of this detail is established around page 50 or so.<br /><br />What happens in the monstrous, monstrous morass that follows? Absolutely fuc*ing nothing.<br />Allegedly, this is a story about a pandemic. In it, the characters sit in meeting rooms talking about things in broad concepts. Reading it is like sitting in those meetings at work that you want to leave. Despite this being a global pandemic, all we get is a mealy-mouthed gesture of academics talking. If what is required for 'hard sci-fi' is this...well, it's just mean spirited. Most of the time, the academics are talking about research funding and room bookings. I couldn't even make it up. It's literally like reading the minute's of your 9-5 pointless job. BUT IT'S REAAL, EDMUND! IT'S REAAL LIFE! If this is real life, I'd rather be dead.<br /><br />The ultimate tell-talk-tell-talk-tell. AND NO SHOW! Somewhere around the 400 page mark, one of the characters looks into a microscope AND DISCOVERS SOMETHING NEW!! Whilst I was amused by how naff a gesture this was, the grasping-at-straws...something...something...is...happening...<br /><br />What does the pandemic do? It makes more babies.<br />Tragic.<br />Why didn't I give it up? At around the 300 page, as if the author has realised how aggressively soporific his book is, people start rioting. <br />Yes, that's right. People are rioting about a pandemic. Eaeargh. <br />There's some issue with abortion that could have been interesting. But since this is the most boring disease ever invented, I'm not entirely sure why they don't just have the babies. <br />Why did I keep going? Well, I thought that the riots might mean anything. But it's indicative of this sludge of a book that the riots all happened 'off-camera', in the end amounted to nothing (they stopped), and the riots themselves WERE ENTIRELY DESCRIBED THROUGH MEETINGS.<br />Pass me a doughnut. Preferably one with cyanide. Not 'Darwin's Radio' cyanide, cyanide that actually does something.<br /><br />Then the president gets blowed up. So what? Why? Exactly.<br />Then two characters have a relationship and the last torrid mile of this ridiculous book is reached. Another character is 'heart broken', and the woman feels bad for him, as if any aspect of their relationship that wasn't two office workers talking. Again, like anything of import that happened in this book, the burning question WHAT ON EARTH IS EVERYBODY SO WORKED UP ABOUT?<br />So, there's some domesticity at the end. It's really boring. But at least it's something.<br /><br />I genuinely felt that this was aggressively dull, and by that I meant that I felt like the author was so keen on writing a 'serious book', they were forcing mundanity, worse than that, down my throat.<br /><br />It's a book that literally made me consider stopping reading entirely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10298997890321078881" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_116619518" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116619518" />
    <a title="Julia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4056360-julia"><img alt="Julia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342803981p2/4056360.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/116619518?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julia" href="/user/show/4056360-julia">Julia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4056360-julia?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer116619518&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116619518" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9069309595291403478">Maybe it's just because I'm an evolutionary biologist, but this book stretched my suspension of disbelief to the breaking point. When something unbelievable happens in a science-fiction book, the author can take one of two approaches: either quickly handwave it with technobabble and move on to focus on the consequences of the event, or foreground the explanation based on reasonable extrapolations of current science. The author tried to do the latter, but his "explanation" made all the sense of a</span>
  <span id="freeText9069309595291403478" style="display:none">Maybe it's just because I'm an evolutionary biologist, but this book stretched my suspension of disbelief to the breaking point. When something unbelievable happens in a science-fiction book, the author can take one of two approaches: either quickly handwave it with technobabble and move on to focus on the consequences of the event, or foreground the explanation based on reasonable extrapolations of current science. The author tried to do the latter, but his "explanation" made all the sense of a handwave.<br /><br />I also found the author's attitudes toward women, particularly the bodily autonomy of women, to be troubling. What happens to the women in this story is a violation of their bodily autonomy: they become pregnant against their wishes. Being disgusted and horrified by this pregnancy is a perfectly normal and understandable reaction. However, by the end of the book, the women who are frightened and repulsed by their unwanted pregnancies and the offspring created of same are vilified, while those who embrace pregnancy and motherhood are celebrated. <br /><br />Not to mention that the children produced by these pregnancies, who are supposed to be yay and wonderful and the next step in human evolution, are just plain <i>creepy</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9069309595291403478" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

And at first that was a challenge. It is definitely hard science fiction.   <span id="freeText6783586435054707199" style="display:none">A re-read for the Powell's SF Book Group in Exile. I got to this book a little later than I planned, so had to push the reading speed. And at first that was a challenge. It is definitely hard science fiction. But I've read a bunch of genetics, dna, anthropology and was able to muscle through - so this ended up being a 2 day read - well 36 hours.<br /><br />Writing near-future hard science fiction that ages well is probably impossible. There's almost no way to get the science right. And for something like this book, in which the science is pretty unlikely.<br /><br />And yet in the end this book was brilliant and interesting, full of possibilities. And with scary echoes of what it's like to be in a pandemic and with odd government responses.<br /><br />I remember the sequel as being quite different but also quite good. So re-reading that goes on the list.</span>

<div id="review_41137545" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41137545" />
    <a title="Lightreads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads"><img alt="Lightreads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1230606449p2/1836077.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41137545?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lightreads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lightreads" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads">Lightreads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41137545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5996653859881115439">A CDC disease chaser discovers a virus that seems to be asymptomatic in everyone but pregnant women, and mass graves in Georgia (the country) and a newly discovered family of forty thousand year old mummies suggest this isn’t the first outbreak. And our heroes -- that CDC disease hound, a successful biologist, and an anthropologist with questionable ethics -- begin to suspect it isn’t an outbreak at all.<br/><br/>Okay, so it’s not actually a ‘read a textbook instead’ science fiction book. I mean, the scie</span>
  <span id="freeText5996653859881115439" style="display:none">A CDC disease chaser discovers a virus that seems to be asymptomatic in everyone but pregnant women, and mass graves in Georgia (the country) and a newly discovered family of forty thousand year old mummies suggest this isn’t the first outbreak. And our heroes -- that CDC disease hound, a successful biologist, and an anthropologist with questionable ethics -- begin to suspect it isn’t an outbreak at all.<br /><br />Okay, so it’s not actually a ‘read a textbook instead’ science fiction book. I mean, the science is pretty cool -- endogenous retroviruses as an evolutionary vector, which is a pretty awesome explanation for the whole “yes but how does it work?” problem of punctuated evolution. And the writing is effective and observant, if a bit clumsy sometimes. Ooh, and there are actual people in this book, with actual people emotions and actual people foibles and actual people joys.<br /><br />But -- you knew it was coming -- I really didn’t like it much. I think it’s that I hate hate hate people who are willfully wrong -- they’ve chosen a path, and okay yeah it’s becoming clear they’re wrong, but hell if they’ll do anything about it. And this book is full of them. I’m sort of torn, actually, because the descriptions of just what scientists and politicians would do faced with a disaster like this one are actually pretty accurate. It’s not a pretty picture, as well it shouldn’t be. But it’s exactly the sort of mess that drives me nuts on a personal level, and it all left a bad taste in my mouth.<br /><br />That, and there’s something really awry with the pacing here. And some weirdness on the boy-girl front I couldn’t quite put my finger on. I hear Bear’s short fiction is more exciting. Hope so.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5996653859881115439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_5826920" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5826920" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/355666-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442474229p2/355666.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5826920?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/355666-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of hard science fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/355666-dan?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5826920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12055825910910969073">Darwin’s Radio is a pleasure for someone who loves hard science fiction, as I do. Here’s the premise: SHEVA, a retrovirus long-buried in our genes, suddenly awakens and begins to attack pregnant women, forcing them to miscarry after three months. But that’s just the beginning – after the miscarriage, these same women spontaneously become pregnant again, this time developing a fetus that’s not quite human. The federal government, led by the science establishment, after first denying the truth, th</span>
  <span id="freeText12055825910910969073" style="display:none">Darwin’s Radio is a pleasure for someone who loves hard science fiction, as I do. Here’s the premise: SHEVA, a retrovirus long-buried in our genes, suddenly awakens and begins to attack pregnant women, forcing them to miscarry after three months. But that’s just the beginning – after the miscarriage, these same women spontaneously become pregnant again, this time developing a fetus that’s not quite human. The federal government, led by the science establishment, after first denying the truth, then begins pressing parents to turn over their strange children to the government. <br /><br /> This premise just blew my mind; it’s creative, believable and terrifying. The science was complex and I referred to the glossary, included at the back of the book, several times. As I progressed through the pages, I was reminded of Beggars in Spain, Nancy Kress’s wonderful story. Both novels explore the rapid evolution of humanity into another species, although Greg Bear, unlike Kress, makes humanity involuntary travelers on the journey. <br /><br />My major complaint is the slow pace. Too much time was spent on a romance between the two major characters. Even more frustrating was the endless politics between and among the scientific community and their patrons. Although Darwin’s Radio is science fiction and not a techno-thriller, more action – yes, a little violence, too – would have strengthened the brew. <br /><br />The bottom line: a slightly flawed but thought-provoking tale. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12055825910910969073" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_152531486" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/152531486" />
    <a title="Kathy Bell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2476537-kathy-bell"><img alt="Kathy Bell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1500254819p2/2476537.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/152531486?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathy Bell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathy Bell" href="/user/show/2476537-kathy-bell">Kathy Bell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152531486" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1746708168978389768">Actually 3.5, were that possible on GoodReads.<br/><br/>I really enjoy science fiction with lots of science, and especially evolutionary concepts, so this book appealed to me immensely in theory. In practice, I found myself skipping huge amounts of text so I could move the plot along. The science behind the concept was intriguing and well developed, but the rest of the story dragged on longer than I thought necessary. For those who like their scifi with indepth descriptions of every character and their ev</span>
  <span id="freeText1746708168978389768" style="display:none">Actually 3.5, were that possible on GoodReads.<br /><br />I really enjoy science fiction with lots of science, and especially evolutionary concepts, so this book appealed to me immensely in theory. In practice, I found myself skipping huge amounts of text so I could move the plot along. The science behind the concept was intriguing and well developed, but the rest of the story dragged on longer than I thought necessary. For those who like their scifi with indepth descriptions of every character and their every action, the book would find a higher rating. <br /><br />This is definitely science fiction, packed with terminology and lab work. But Bear did a good job making it accessible to non-scifi buffs with the glossary and diffusion of the science throughout the human interest portion of the story. Personally, I preferred the science portion, finding the relationships a little shallow and quick to form.<br /><br />I do look forward to reading the sequel, to find out more about the new species of human.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1746708168978389768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3339925566" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3339925566" />
    <a title="drowningmermaid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2008190-drowningmermaid"><img alt="drowningmermaid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1254467567p2/2008190.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3339925566?book_show_action=true">May 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="drowningmermaid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="drowningmermaid" href="/user/show/2008190-drowningmermaid">drowningmermaid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2008190-drowningmermaid?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2008190-drowningmermaid?shelf=audio">audio</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3339925566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4963953779339072024">A frustrating book that hovers just on the edge of brilliance and instead settles for 'incomprehensible.' I mean, it would be awesome if the themes of religion and religious-archetypes-as-harbingers-of-major-subspeciation-events was explored. It would be awesome if the idea that 'evolution selects for individuals who could bring about major evolutionary events' was explored. Using the unexplained tools of evolution-- like desire and love. Or war.<br/><br/>It would be awesome if <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[people's fac</span></span>
  <span id="freeText4963953779339072024" style="display:none">A frustrating book that hovers just on the edge of brilliance and instead settles for 'incomprehensible.' I mean, it would be awesome if the themes of religion and religious-archetypes-as-harbingers-of-major-subspeciation-events was explored. It would be awesome if the idea that 'evolution selects for individuals who could bring about major evolutionary events' was explored. Using the unexplained tools of evolution-- like desire and love. Or war.<br /><br />It would be awesome if <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[people's faces falling off  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> meant something or symbolized something or in some way correlated with anything. But srsly why was that bit in there?<br /><br />It feels like this wants to be a fascinating literary work that explores the role of faith and macro-organisms and societal organisms in the process of evolution. Are the dreams expressed by the hero with a thousand faces a reflection of our evolutionary lineage-- and future? And was our evolution then triggered by a conscious mind that will drive us to ultimately arrive at an endpoint? An ultimate level of sentience that can be welcomed into an interstellar consortium, say, or prepare our planet for colonization, or grow us into a race that has long been lost?<br /><br />Instead we have two scientists banging enthusiastically while the world burns. And a whole bunch of conversations about how no one is going to believe that evolution contains "Mind" within junk DNA. <br /><br />It kind of doesn't help to be reading this in quarantine. There's a weird vilification of mask-wearing that feels weird right now. There's a scene where a riot breaks out in a march put on by peaceful women when news breaks of a single dead baby. That 1) makes very little sense after the Women's March and 2) makes even less sense when you've seen enough protests to know that unless there is footage of a murder, there isn't likely to be a riot.<br /><br />The Bill Cosby interview didn't age well, either.<br /><br />If a movie were made of this, I definitely want Mitchell to be black. Hollywood needs some blackwash and this character has no physical description and I feel like a lot of the love triangle would be more compelling with an African American anthropological scientist.<br /><br />Mostly, it was okay. A miss for me, but okay.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4963953779339072024" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_904883150" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/904883150" />
    <a title="Faith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9571330-faith"><img alt="Faith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446127921p2/9571330.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/904883150?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Faith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Faith" href="/user/show/9571330-faith">Faith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9571330-faith?shelf=owned-hardcopy">owned-hardcopy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9571330-faith?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9571330-faith?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer904883150" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2146326303351780300">Oh man. This was basically a DNF, as I did a lot of skimming.<br/><br/>I picked this up expecting a sort of virus-thriller (like Contagion, or the Andromeda Strain, I imagine). And besides, it was about things hiding in our genes and I have a keen academic interest in evolution.<br/><br/>But I could NOT get through this.<br/><br/>Firstly, the science. Bear actually started out solidly, talking about lysogenic viruses and how our genome could contain parts of these viruses. Okay, so far so good. Suddenly they could be activat</span>
  <span id="freeText2146326303351780300" style="display:none">Oh man. This was basically a DNF, as I did a lot of skimming.<br /><br />I picked this up expecting a sort of virus-thriller (like Contagion, or the Andromeda Strain, I imagine). And besides, it was about things hiding in our genes and I have a keen academic interest in evolution.<br /><br />But I could NOT get through this.<br /><br />Firstly, the science. Bear actually started out solidly, talking about lysogenic viruses and how our genome could contain parts of these viruses. Okay, so far so good. Suddenly they could be activated, making viruses again! Ah scary! Cool, with you so far. Hey! They could also be somehow involved in evolution, by transferring genetic material laterally instead of vertically, like some bacteria do. Okay, interesting.<br /><br />And maybe that evolution is somehow directed, and the virus has been triggered because our species needs to evolve RIGHT NOW into something new.<br /><br />Okay....what?<br /><br />Evolution does not work like that. At all. Even proponents of punctuated equilibrium don't imagine that speciation occurs within a single generation, as far as I'm aware.<br /><br />I recognize that this is a work of fiction. There's nothing that says you have to use real science in your book, and I'm certainly not going to insist that science fiction be written my way or not at all.<br /><br />But this just wasn't my cup of tea.<br /><br />Setting aside the science, though, there are still problems. The pace is <i>slow</i>, and includes plot points and characters that to me felt extraneous. I was also looking forward to reading about a brilliant lady scientist, but eventually the book became all about the new babies and not so much about science, and lady scientists. <br /><br />Also I thought the form of the "new species" was ridiculous, but I recognize that's an issue of personal taste.<br /><br />Ultimately, just not my thing. Off it goes to BookMooch, where maybe it can make someone else happy.<br /><br />Oh, one last thing. Bear called Neandertals humans! I was so excited! All ready to give him brownie points<br /><br />Then I realized where he was headed, with the whole Neandertal-parents-modern-baby thing. Yeah, no. SO MUCH NO. I just...NO. <br /><br />(I'm not going to go into a full explanation here, but if you're curious please ask. Please! I love talking about ancient humans and early hominins).</span>
  <a data-text-id="2146326303351780300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3333614253" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3333614253" />
    <a title="Jacob" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/901342-jacob"><img alt="Jacob" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555491633p2/901342.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3333614253?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob" href="/user/show/901342-jacob">Jacob</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9113820-darwin-s-radio">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=from_library">from_library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=science_fiction">science_fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=powells_sf_group">powells_sf_group</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3333614253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6702120989822911099">Maybe this book and I got off to a bad start. I had no idea what it was about, and when I started reading about this guy stuck in a frozen land and none of the names were recognizable I thought the guy was on a distant planet that was mostly frozen. Then there started to be some kind of semi-related creatures discovered, and I thought they must be aliens, and was Darwin’s Radio something broadcasting through space? Then it finally became clear this was all taking place on Earth and everyone invo</span>
  <span id="freeText6702120989822911099" style="display:none">Maybe this book and I got off to a bad start. I had no idea what it was about, and when I started reading about this guy stuck in a frozen land and none of the names were recognizable I thought the guy was on a distant planet that was mostly frozen. Then there started to be some kind of semi-related creatures discovered, and I thought they must be aliens, and was Darwin’s Radio something broadcasting through space? Then it finally became clear this was all taking place on Earth and everyone involved so far was a scientist. So maybe this story is interesting, and I feel gypped only because I thought the story was going to be one thing and then it was completely different.<br /><br />This is actually a pretty standard hard science fiction novel, if Kim Stanley Robinson and Carl Sagan are any guide:<br /><br />500+ pages? Check.<br />Long page count because all kinds of plodding details of committees and decisions and policy are discussed? Check.<br />You can see the point coming for hundreds of pages and it’s kind of a slog to wait for the characters to finally see it too? Check.<br />An intimate look at the not-as-exciting-as-you’d-think-even-in-fiction daily lives of scientists? Check.<br />Political decisions that don’t make a ton of sense? Check.<br /><br />Unfortunately, not all of the phenomenon being imagined here makes sense or pays off (weird thick skin masks?). The love triangle is kind of good, except that it takes a hard left turn and ends with a gratuitous bombing (less satisfactory than you would think). I think hard science fiction that takes place on Earth just isn’t my thing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6702120989822911099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27125803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8270565308574028801">There is a marvelous skinny book inside this sluggish fat monster longing to get out: no more than 200 pages, please!<br/><br/>Its at times like these that I understand the True Value of Reader's Digest Condensed Books. Trim it down by half, cut out the turgid subplots, the rambling characters who drift in and out!<br/><br/>And yet started so well ... taut and crisp, it tingled the curiosity and fired the imagination ... only to spiral down into ever increasing, never ceasing scientific ramblings and soap opera sub</span>
  <span id="freeText8270565308574028801" style="display:none">There is a marvelous skinny book inside this sluggish fat monster longing to get out: no more than 200 pages, please!<br /><br />Its at times like these that I understand the True Value of Reader's Digest Condensed Books. Trim it down by half, cut out the turgid subplots, the rambling characters who drift in and out!<br /><br />And yet started so well ... taut and crisp, it tingled the curiosity and fired the imagination ... only to spiral down into ever increasing, never ceasing scientific ramblings and soap opera sub plots.<br /><br />Half way through I had lost sympathy for the characters, and that separate analytical part which I like to suspend when reading mysteries and science fiction started to pick holes and make unflattering commentary. So I speed read the monster grabbing bits of paragraphs here and there until the final. dissatisfying chapter and it's lame epilogue ... <br /><br />And he most dissatisfying part of all is that I could see the makings of a fabulous story in here, with great science and a really interesting twist on evolution.<br /><br />damn.<br /><br />Read at your peril.</span>

<div id="review_423558744" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/423558744" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1241024-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/423558744?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/1241024-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer423558744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8299695961759593022">This sci-fi "thriller" never really worked for me. The basic premise -- there is a virus that allows the human genetic code to undergo a massive change to a higher lifeform -- provides a reasonable basis for the novel, but as a story it never comes together.<br/><br/>The details of the new virus slowly emerge, as more people become infected and scientists start to study it. Instead of building dramatically in the novel, it gets slowly dished out with enough hints as to where it is going that I started to </span>
  <span id="freeText8299695961759593022" style="display:none">This sci-fi "thriller" never really worked for me. The basic premise -- there is a virus that allows the human genetic code to undergo a massive change to a higher lifeform -- provides a reasonable basis for the novel, but as a story it never comes together.<br /><br />The details of the new virus slowly emerge, as more people become infected and scientists start to study it. Instead of building dramatically in the novel, it gets slowly dished out with enough hints as to where it is going that I started to feel as though I had read each detail three times. Ultimately, I just stopped caring.<br /><br />The characters started off seeming relatively interesting, but no one in the book acted like a real person. The widespread riots and reactions in the book seemed ridiculous to me, and the main characters faced no bigger enemies than bureaucracy and their own indecision.<br /><br />When a sci-fi epidemic book is more mundane and less dramatic than any number of books that tell true stories of viral outbreaks, why waste your time?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8299695961759593022" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1699668687" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1699668687" />
    <a title="Maree" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5001811-maree"><img alt="Maree" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304369647p2/5001811.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1699668687?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maree" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maree" href="/user/show/5001811-maree">Maree</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1699668687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15429215904225418475">I've been trying to figure out if I've read this one before, or if it just seems familiar because I've read the second book in the series. I think I remember the second book being a lot more engaging, perhaps because there are a lot of very smart children in it, and who doesn't enjoy little kids showing up adults?<br/><br/>This start is a little drier, and it's very science heavy, so if you're not into DNA and genetics, you might want to skip this one. I have a passing interest, and I was decently able to</span>
  <span id="freeText15429215904225418475" style="display:none">I've been trying to figure out if I've read this one before, or if it just seems familiar because I've read the second book in the series. I think I remember the second book being a lot more engaging, perhaps because there are a lot of very smart children in it, and who doesn't enjoy little kids showing up adults?<br /><br />This start is a little drier, and it's very science heavy, so if you're not into DNA and genetics, you might want to skip this one. I have a passing interest, and I was decently able to follow it with my college courses, but some of it was definitely over my head as well. And when it wasn't getting into the nitty gritty of what is a virus and what is not, it was talking about the spread and trying to scientifically figure out gestation and other fun science-y things. Which I did like, but is definitely meant for a specific audience.<br /><br />It did feel like it was just getting started at the end there, so book 2 is the place to be! I may have to reread that one.</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17094577724688321794">This book could have been considerably better, but the execution was just way off. The idea is interesting, but has significant overtones of the misunderstanding that evolution is in some way directed towards improvement - more really needs to be done to emphasize why this is not a problem, otherwise you're going to lose me in magical thinking.<br/><br/>The other issue is that the story and the characters don't seem particularly compelling, and the whole book kinda goes nowhere. I found jarring <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoi</a></span>
  <span id="freeText17094577724688321794" style="display:none">This book could have been considerably better, but the execution was just way off. The idea is interesting, but has significant overtones of the misunderstanding that evolution is in some way directed towards improvement - more really needs to be done to emphasize why this is not a problem, otherwise you're going to lose me in magical thinking.<br /><br />The other issue is that the story and the characters don't seem particularly compelling, and the whole book kinda goes nowhere. I found jarring <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the speed at which David and Kaye got together, as nothing in their interactions prior to that seemed all that romantic, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> though that would honestly be easily forgivable if it went anywhere interesting.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer199777356" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16180208114136141669">I have decided Greg Bear is just not a writer I can enjoy.<br/><br/>His books are full of characters to whom things just <i>happen.</i> They don't act as much as flail around, buffeted by forces they don't understand, until finally the story just stops, right in time for the sequel hook, which is probably the part of the book that the author has been thinking about since page 20.<br/><br/>So, instead of trying to read Darwin's Children and see if there is actually some fire behind all this smoke, I am going to stop wastin</span>
  <span id="freeText16180208114136141669" style="display:none">I have decided Greg Bear is just not a writer I can enjoy.<br /><br />His books are full of characters to whom things just <i>happen.</i> They don't act as much as flail around, buffeted by forces they don't understand, until finally the story just stops, right in time for the sequel hook, which is probably the part of the book that the author has been thinking about since page 20.<br /><br />So, instead of trying to read Darwin's Children and see if there is actually some fire behind all this smoke, I am going to stop wasting my time, and read actual books with actual characters that actually do things that actually have some effect on their lives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16180208114136141669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('EERj43kZAG7D0MqA1GlDxtNeyo2rkS+vdRSxEooQb10zU8iATCfKReXW6cN3B4yB+bBbYG+iZmfpAH+xc6aA1g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2udG+ypdPtbuAmBRyyCixnBLs06tXZhEGtMbNcdjRVr58O2YH2P0/cgEQxJoTm2BWqUio2lu0YyGx9WWPtWq0Q==')})
  }
//]]>

Not only did the wormhole drop him in the middle of a civil war over a depleted resource, but the battle is actually taking place inside his father’s brain. Bernard has one chance to save the dying side of his father’s creative brain from the tyrannical left side. Can he use his outside-the-box thinking to save his father’s life?\n\nBrainwalker is a young adult sci-fi fantasy novel that turns the world of neuroscience on its head. <span id="freeTextContainer11615315340460291213">Greg Bear is one of the world's leading hard SF authors. He sold his first short story, at the age of fifteen, to Robert Lowndes's Famous Science Fiction. <br/><br/>A full-time writer, he lives in Washington State with his family. He is married to Astrid Anderson Bear. He is the son-in-law of Poul Anderson. They are the parents of two children, Erik and Alexandra.<br/><br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://us.macmillan.com/author/gregbear'>http://us.macmillan.com/author/gregbear</a></span>

Now these changed children have reached adolescence . . . and face a world that is outraged about their very existence. For these special youths, possessed of remarkable, advanced traits that mark a major turning point in human development, are also ticking time bombs harboring hosts of viruses that could exterminate the “old” human race.\n\nFear and hatred of the virus children have made them a persecuted underclass, quarantined by the government in special “schools,” targeted by federally sanctioned bounty hunters, and demonized by hysterical segments of the population. But pockets of resistance have sprung up among those opposed to treating the children like dangerous diseases—and who fear the worst if the government’s draconian measures are carried to their extreme.\n\nScientists Kaye Lang and Mitch Rafelson are part of this small but determined minority. Once at the forefront of the discovery and study of the SHEVA outbreak, they now live as virtual exiles in the Virginia suburbs with their daughter, Stella—a bright, inquisitive virus child who is quickly maturing, straining to break free of the protective world her parents have built around her, and eager to seek out others of her kind.\n\nBut for all their precautions, Kaye, Mitch, and Stella have not slipped below the government’s radar. The agencies fanatically devoted to segregating and controlling the new-breed children monitor their every move—watching and waiting for the opportunity to strike the next blow in their escalating war
